Special Report: Trump and Xi give closing comments during U.S.-China summit

NBC News(11.9M) ·29:59 ·241.0K views · 2026-05-15
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Xi Jinping (President of China)
President Trump and President Xi concluded a high-stakes summit in Beijing, signaling a shift toward diplomatic stability and "fantastic" trade prospects—including potential multi-billion dollar Boeing and energy deals—while navigating deep-seated tensions over Taiwan and Iran.
TL;DR President Trump and President Xi concluded a high-stakes summit in Beijing, signaling a shift toward diplomatic stability and "fantastic" trade prospects—including potential multi-billion dollar Boeing and energy deals—while navigating deep-seated tensions over Taiwan and...

TL;DR

President Trump and President Xi concluded a high-stakes summit in Beijing, signaling a shift toward diplomatic stability and "fantastic" trade prospects—including potential multi-billion dollar Boeing and energy deals—while navigating deep-seated tensions over Taiwan and Iran.

Diplomatic Atmosphere and Symbolism

  • Venue Significance: President Xi hosted the meetings at the Zhongnanhai compound, the historic residence of Chinese leadership since 1949.
      • The choice of venue was a reciprocal gesture for the hospitality Xi received at Mar-a-Lago in 2017.
      • The leaders engaged in "tea diplomacy" and toured the grounds, which feature trees up to 1,000 years old.
  • Personal Rapport: President Trump characterized the relationship as "stronger than ever before," describing Xi as a "friend" of nearly 12 years and a leader he "respects greatly."
  • Reciprocity: A follow-up state visit was announced; President Xi is scheduled to visit the United States on or around September 24th.

Trade and Economic Commitments

  • Major Trade Deals: Trump claimed "fantastic trade deals" were reached that would benefit both nations, though specific contractual details were not immediately released.
  • Aerospace and Energy:
      • Boeing: Trump suggested China may purchase 150 to 200 Boeing jets, though earlier reports speculated the number could reach 500.
      • Energy Exports: Discussions included China purchasing oil and natural gas from Texas, Louisiana, and Alaska to help the U.S. become a dominant global energy player.
  • CEO Delegation: The summit featured a "powerhouse" business delegation intended to project U.S. economic strength and secure market access.
      • Nvidia: CEO Jensen Huang emphasized his focus on representing U.S. interests and supporting the President.
      • Apple: CEO Tim Cook cited a goal of "great collaboration" between U.S. tech and Chinese markets.
      • Tesla: Elon Musk attended high-level meetings with Chinese Premier Li Qiang.

Geopolitical Security and Regional Stability

  • Iran Nuclear Stance: Both leaders agreed that Iran must not be allowed to possess nuclear weapons.
      • Strait of Hormuz: There is a mutual interest in reopening the straits to ensure the flow of commerce and energy.
      • Military Equipment: Trump claimed Xi committed to ensuring Chinese companies do not provide military equipment to Iran, despite reports of potential third-party "loopholes."
  • Taiwan Tensions: President Xi used the summit to issue "frank and stark" warnings regarding Taiwan, which China views as a domestic issue.
      • Arms Sales: Tensions remain high as the U.S. Congress has approved $14 billion in arms sales to Taiwan, currently awaiting the President’s signature.
      • Semiconductor Factor: Analysts noted Taiwan’s role as the "world capital of microchip production" remains a critical commercial subtext for both nations.

Strategic Outlook and Risks

  • Projecting Stability: For China, the summit’s primary goal was to project global stability and validate Xi's status as a global statesman.
  • Fragile Progress: Analysts warned that despite the cordial "chocolate cake" moments, the relationship remains adversarial and fragile.
      • Historical precedents, such as the 2017 summit, show that trade wars and volatility can quickly override diplomatic goodwill.
  • Media Environment: The summit was highly controlled; Chinese state media emphasized mutual cooperation, while avoiding the "rogue moments" or aggressive press questioning typical of White House briefings.
"We've made some fantastic trade deals, great for both countries. [Xi] is a man I respect greatly, become really a friend... we've settled a lot of different problems that other people wouldn't have been able to settle." — President Donald Trump
"This visit is a historic and landmark visit. So far, we have reached a new bilateral relationship, which is a constructive strategic relationship. This can be said to be a milestone visit event." — President Xi Jinping
"We discussed Iran. We feel very similar on Iran. We want that to end. We don't want them to have a nuclear weapon. We want the straits open." — President Donald Trump

And we've settled a lot of different problems that other people wouldn't have been able to settle. And the relationship is a very strong one. We've really done some wonderful things, I believe. The $10 Billion Hunt for the Rocks That Power the World

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M) ·12:13 ·69.3K views · 2026-05-15
Donald Trump (US President) Xi Jinping (President of China)
China’s near-total monopoly on the rare earth supply chain (80-90%) has become a critical geopolitical "bazooka," forcing a $10 billion global scramble led by the U.S.
TL;DR China’s near-total monopoly on the rare earth supply chain (80-90%) has become a critical geopolitical "bazooka," forcing a $10 billion global scramble led by the U.S. and allies to secure independent mining, processing, and magnet manufacturing. The 2025 Trade War:...

TL;DR

China’s near-total monopoly on the rare earth supply chain (80-90%) has become a critical geopolitical "bazooka," forcing a $10 billion global scramble led by the U.S. and allies to secure independent mining, processing, and magnet manufacturing.

The Geopolitical "Rare Earth Bazooka"

  • The 2025 Trade War: Following the return of Donald Trump to the White House and the imposition of 145% tariffs on Chinese goods, Beijing retaliated with export controls on rare earth elements.
  • Economic Vulnerability: The U.S. automotive industry warned of total factory shutdowns if permanent magnets—essential for electric motors—remained blocked by Chinese controls.
  • Historical Leverage: This mirrors the 2010 territorial dispute with Japan, where China restricted exports to cripple the Japanese automotive sector, providing an early warning of the risks of single-source dependency.

The Processing and Manufacturing Chokepoint

  • Control Beyond Mining: While China holds the largest deposits, its true dominance lies in downstream processing.
      • Refining: China controls 90% of the chemical separation and refining process.
      • Magnet Production: China controls roughly 94% of the global production of rare earth permanent magnets.
  • Environmental Outsourcing: Western nations historically ceded this industry to China due to the highly toxic and radioactive waste produced during the refining stage.
  • The Talent Gap: A significant barrier to Western competition is specialized labor; China has dedicated universities for mining and engineering with 25,000 students, while the U.S. graduated only 350 mining engineers last year.

National Security and the Iran War

  • Depleted Munitions: The U.S. military has rapidly consumed its stockpiles of advanced weapon systems during the Iran War, using more than half of its pre-war inventory of certain high-cost munitions.
  • Defense Necessity: Rare earths are essential for the guidance systems and sensors in these weapons, making the replenishment of stockpiles impossible without a secure supply chain.
  • Strategic Red Lines: China has specifically banned the export of rare earths for use in U.S. weapons, labeling their use in defense as an "illegal diversion."

The $10 Billion Global Pivot

  • Western Investment: An estimated $10 billion in public funds will be invested in non-Chinese rare earth ventures in 2026 to "backstop" companies against Chinese price manipulation.
  • Key Market Players:
      • Lynas (Australia): The leading producer outside China; it recently broke the monopoly on "heavy" rare earths and holds a $96 million U.S. defense contract.
      • MP Materials (USA): The Pentagon took a $400 million stake in this company in 2025 to accelerate domestic heavy rare earth separation.
      • Meteoric Resources (Brazil): Developing a 1.5-billion-ton rare earth clay deposit, positioning Brazil as a major sustainable alternative to Chinese ore.
  • Long-term Outlook: Experts estimate that while the monopoly won't be broken in five years, the goal of a 50/50 supply split between China and the West is achievable by the early 2030s.

Notable Quotes

"The Middle East has its oil, but China has its rare earth." — Deng Xiaoping, Former Paramount Leader of China
"We broke the monopoly on separated heavy rare earths last year in May 2025. We are the only producer at scale of both separated lights and separated heavies outside of China." — Representative, Lynas
"The automotive industry came out and not so quietly said we are going to have to shut down factories if we cannot get these permanent magnets." — Bloomberg Narrative
"This is a story about not letting one country hold all the chips on what is a very important supply chain." — Bloomberg Narrative
It doesn't look like much, but this is the prize for the rest of the world in trying to catch up with China. We need rare earths. China owns them, processes them, refines them, and sells them. The entire supply chain is built around Beijing. No matter what you do, you're always kind of beholden to China. Rare earths are really the backbone of modern technology. China's the ballgame. They're 80 to 90% of production and processing of rare earths that go into all equipment around the world. And China's shown it will use that dominance as geopolitical leverage. The main use of rare earths are in these powerful high-performance magnets. China's capacity for magnets is vast compared to the rest of the world. When Donald Trump returned to the White House in 2025 and rebooted the US-China trade war, China responded with export controls on rare earths that exposed just how much Western manufacturing still depends on China's supply. The automotive industry came out and not so quietly said we are going to have to shut down factories if we cannot get these permanent magnets. That shock set off a broader scramble to build a new supply chain from mine to magnet. This is a story about not letting one country hold all the chips on what is a very important supply chain. The US-China trade war has begun. In case you'd forgotten how influential rare earths are, it was little more than a year ago that they forced a pause in the US-China trade war. We have a 145% tariff on Chinese goods; neither one wants to be the one to pick up the phone first. In April 2025, US President Donald Trump slapped huge tariffs on Chinese imports. Beijing fought back, pulling out its rare earth bazooka. The automotive CEOs from Detroit were all showing up, telling Donald Trump that if you do not get those permanent magnets, which are made from the rare earths, moving back into the United States, we're going to have to start shutting factories. Beijing had located the weak spot, and at the center of it was a group of metals most people never think about. Rare earths are a group of 17 metallic elements that are chemically very similar. They can seem obscure, but the important thing is what they do. They help create powerful, heat-resistant magnets. They're used in, you know, most of our modern technologies, especially in electric motors. They make our gadgets smaller, lighter and more powerful. From phones and wind turbines to electric vehicles and missile systems, rare earth magnets sit inside much of modern technology. And that means the global high-tech economy relies on one country. China's home to the world's largest rare earth deposit that's been discovered so far. Specifically, it has deposits of heavy rare earths, whereas the US has very, very little. Heavy rare earths are the most expensive and sought after. They allow high-performance magnets to resist heat and demagnetization. The real choke point is the processing stage. China controls 90% of the refining process, and then it goes to magnet production, which is the process of making rare earth metals into magnets. China controls about 94% of that as well. Mining the ore is only the start. The real work is in the chemistry, separating and refining each element into something magnet makers can use. The production is so costly and also environmentally very damaging because the processing stage, you know, produces a lot of toxic waste including radioactive materials. So a lot of Western countries, they're happy to let China take over. And you can see why. This is Baotou in Inner Mongolia, home to China's largest rare earth production site. Wherever you are in the world, at some level of that supply chain, you are probably reliant on Chinese products. With China holding such a dominant position, why did it come as such a shock to the Trump administration when they chose to use it? It really shouldn't have been, because China used the exact leverage 15 years ago. Perhaps because last time it wasn't the US in the crosshairs; it was Japan. China first used its dominance over rare earths as a geopolitical weapon in 2010 when they restricted exports to Japan because of a territorial dispute. China said, okay, we are going to restrict the exports of our rare earths that you need, Japan, to turn into the permanent magnets that go into your automotive industry and other industries. This was the first kind of shock to the market. It lasted only weeks. Even so, it exposed Japan's vulnerability and offered an early warning of how much leverage China had built. To understand how that happened, we have to rewind. Actually, the US was the biggest player in rare earths all the way from the 1950s to the 1980s. China is a latecomer in the industry, but it caught up really fast. This was not an accident. Beijing treated rare earths as a strategic industry and built capacity all the way down the chain. China's late paramount leader Deng Xiaoping had said famously that the Middle East has its oil, but China has its rare earth. As China scaled up, it became cheaper to buy from there than to make it anywhere else. In the West, thin margins, high labor costs and dirty processes pushed many operators out. Consumers were happy with the status quo because they got a steady flow of cheap, reliable supplies of magnets from China for many, many years. But now the risks of relying so much on China are clear, forcing countries to make their own rare earth plays. This is the largest rare earth processing site outside of China. It's run by the Australian mining company Lynas, which has a 96 million dollar contract to supply heavy rare earths to the US Department of Defense. Lynas is really the leader in the wave of companies now trying to rebuild the rare earth supply chain outside of China. They started more than a decade ago; it took them a long time to start ramping up production. Lynas aims to produce over 10,000 tons of rare earth oxides per year from this site. But most importantly, it's worked out how to produce heavy rare earths. We broke the monopoly on separated heavy rare earths last year in May 2025. We are the only producer at scale of both separated lights and separated heavies outside of China. Rebuilding a supply chain this specialized also means rebuilding skills, experience and industrial muscle. Last year in the US, they graduated 35,000 lawyers. They graduated 350 mining engineers. Meanwhile, China has a dedicated mining and engineering university with 25,000 undergraduate students. China has invested in developing competence in the rare earths market and getting better and being more efficient every day. And the urgency for the US has only grown as its war with Iran has depleted the Pentagon's advanced weapon stocks. We've seen the US burning through its stockpiles of defense systems and weapon systems really quickly. And the US will need to replenish these stockpiles, and to make weapons and defense systems, it requires rare earths. The US military's exact demand is classified, but a recent report by the Center for Strategic and International Studies estimates during the Iran war, the US has used more than half of its pre-war inventory of some very expensive munitions. There is definitely a sense of urgency, and it's not just the United States. It is the Western world. If countries want an alternative to China, they need more than new factories. They also need new deposits. And Brazil is one of the main bets. Latin America's biggest economy is home to roughly a quarter of the world's rare earths, according to the US Geological Survey. This site in southeastern Brazil is run by another Australian developer, Meteoric Resources, that's also receiving backing from the US. The company says there are 1.5 billion tons of rare earth clay in these hills. Our main objective right now is to put the project in production, to be the next to the world to produce the mixed rare earth carbonate and to develop the technology to do the separation. And we can do that differently as China does right now. If you want to be sustainable, you have to buy from a sustainable market. And the US is also investing at home. Their biggest hope is the Pentagon-backed MP Materials. The Department of Defense bought a 400 million dollar stake in the company in 2025. It expects to be able to separate heavy rare earths later this year. But the problem for every would-be challenger: China's big enough to squeeze margins long before new competitors are secure. The Western world are intervening and saying that they're willing to put up money to build out these companies, to effectively be a partner that says we're going to backstop any chance that China takes to flush you out of the market. China's restrictions on rare earth exports to the US have been relaxing, but its ban on exports for use in US weapons is a red line. The objective is to prevent the illegal diversion of rare earths toward improper uses, such as weapons of mass destruction. I think the main lesson we can draw is that building up the supply chain takes time and a lot of efforts. Japan's dependence on China only reduced from 90% in 2010 to about 60 to 70% now. So you can tell that the 15-year process, it's long and it's slow. If you're a company that wants 60% from China, 40% from the rest of the world, or 50/50, that's a more realizable goal up to 2030 and into the early next decade. A Bloomberg Intelligence report estimates 10 billion dollars of public funds will be invested in rare earth ventures outside of China in 2026. That's a big turnaround. The monopoly is not going to be broken within the next five years. But the US, Australia, Brazil, Japan, South Korea, France will all begin producing some commercial scale of permanent magnets of rare earths. And if you're sitting here five years from now and those nations are all producing stuff that actually goes out the door to major buyers, then that's a win.

TL;DR

President Trump and President Xi’s high-stakes Beijing summit yielded pledges from China to assist in reopening the Strait of Hormuz and a major Boeing aircraft deal, while the U.S. simultaneously engaged in critical "aid diplomacy" with a crisis-stricken Cuba.

The Beijing Summit: Global Security and Stability

  • Iran and the Strait of Hormuz: Secretary of State Marco Rubio confirmed that President Trump pressed China to use its influence as Iran’s largest ally to stabilize the region.
      • Military Pledges: President Xi reportedly pledged that China would not provide military equipment to the Iranian regime.
      • Strait Access: China expressed opposition to the "militarization" of the Strait of Hormuz and the implementation of an Iranian tolling system for commercial vessels.
      • Nuclear Red Line: Rubio emphasized that the U.S. position remains firm: Iran cannot be allowed to acquire a nuclear weapon, which would give them permanent leverage over global energy corridors.
  • Taiwan Tensions: Despite the "partner, not rival" rhetoric at the state dinner, Chinese officials warned that the relationship could "collide" if the Taiwan issue is mishandled.
      • U.S. Stance: Secretary Rubio stated that U.S. policy remains unchanged and the U.S. will continue to defend Taiwan’s status, though China prefers a "voluntary" unification.
  • Human Rights: President Trump raised the case of Jimmy Lai, the pro-democracy publisher currently imprisoned in Hong Kong. The U.S. is seeking his release and would be open to him relocating to America.

Economic Deliverables and Trade

  • Major Manufacturing Deals: The administration is touting significant "wins" for American jobs, most notably an order for 200 Boeing jets.
      • Agricultural Gains: Commitments were made for increased Chinese purchases of U.S. beef and soybeans.
      • Energy Exports: There is growing Chinese interest in purchasing more U.S. oil and gas to replace or supplement Iranian supplies.
  • CEO Diplomacy: The summit featured a high-profile "all-star" business delegation including Tim Cook (Apple), Jensen Huang (Nvidia), and Elon Musk (Tesla/X).
      • Access: Chinese Premier Li Qiang told the CEOs that "China’s door will only open wider" to American business interests.

Diplomatic Crisis and Engagement in Cuba

  • High-Level Negotiations: CIA Director John Ratcliffe met with Cuban officials in Havana to deliver a message: the U.S. will only engage on economic issues if "fundamental changes" are made to the regime.
  • $100 Million Aid Package: The U.S. offered $100 million in food and medicine to combat the island’s total collapse of the electrical grid and fuel supplies.
      • Distribution Conditions: Rubio specified that the aid must be distributed by non-governmental organizations, such as the Catholic Church, to ensure it reaches the people rather than the government.
      • Regime Acceptance: After initially calling the offer a "lie," Cuban leader Miguel Díaz-Canel officially posted a statement accepting the aid.

Domestic Briefing and General News

  • Hantavirus Monitoring: The CDC is monitoring travelers from an infected cruise ship. While one initial test was a false positive, officials are keeping high-risk individuals in quarantine; current public risk is considered "low."
  • Alex Murdaugh Legal Twist: The South Carolina Supreme Court overturned Murdaugh’s double-murder convictions due to jury tampering by a clerk of court. A new trial has been ordered.
  • Missing Person Investigation: The U.S. Coast Guard seized the "Soulmate" vessel 40 miles off the Florida coast. The boat belongs to Brian Hooker, whose wife, Lynette, vanished in the Bahamas weeks ago.
  • Panda Diplomacy: Two new giant pandas, Fu Shuang and Ping Ping, are being prepared for transfer from Chengdu to Zoo Atlanta as part of a 10-year conservation agreement.

Trump-Xi Summit, Benioff: "Not My First SaaSpocalypse," OpenAI vs Apple, Multi-Sensory AI, El Niño

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·76:31 ·208.2K views · 2026-05-15
Marc Benioff (CEO, Salesforce) Donald Trump (US President) Xi Jinping (President of China)
> "I’m not a Democrat or a Republican.
TL;DR: Salesforce CEO Marc Benioff and the "Besties" analyze the Trump-Xi summit's focus on economic entanglement as a deterrent to war, while addressing the "SaaSpocalypse" market rerating and the shift toward multi-sensory, agentic AI models. Strategic Entanglement: The...

TL;DR: Salesforce CEO Marc Benioff and the "Besties" analyze the Trump-Xi summit's focus on economic entanglement as a deterrent to war, while addressing the "SaaSpocalypse" market rerating and the shift toward multi-sensory, agentic AI models.

U.S.-China Relations: Peace Through Trade

  • Strategic Entanglement: The Trump-Xi summit focused on "economic entanglement" as a primary tool for peace.
      • Trade Deals: China committed to purchasing 200 Boeing jets, U.S. oil, LNG, and massive quantities of soybeans via Cargill.
      • The "Board of Trade" Philosophy: Benioff emphasized that business is the "greatest platform for change," suggesting that deeper financial ties reduce the likelihood of conflict.
  • The Taiwan Issue: The panel debated the future of Taiwan as a geopolitical flashpoint.
      • Diminishing Strategic Value: David Friedberg argued that as both the U.S. (via TSMC in Arizona) and China mainland their chip fabrication, the strategic necessity of Taiwan may decrease by 2040.
      • Chip Sales: Chamath Palihapitiya advocated for continuing to sell high-end chips to China to ensure U.S. companies like Nvidia maintain market dominance over domestic Chinese competitors like Huawei.
  • Corporate Diplomacy: Prominent CEOs (Elon Musk, Tim Cook, Jensen Huang) are serving as unofficial ambassadors, leveraging their unique access to President Xi to maintain business stability.

The "SaaSpocalypse" and Software's AI Pivot

  • Market Rerating: Enterprise software stocks (Salesforce, ServiceNow, Workday) have faced 37-45% declines.
      • Valuation Shift: Benioff noted that companies like HubSpot are trading at unprecedented lows (2x sales), describing this as a "hypnosis around AI" where the market is waiting for productivity gains to manifest in financial results.
      • Internal Efficiency: Salesforce is spending $300M on Anthropic tokens to automate coding and support, significantly reducing development costs.
  • The Rise of Agents: The focus of SaaS is shifting from human-operated software to "Agentforce"—autonomous agents that handle customer interactions and BDR (Business Development Representative) functions.
      • The Semantic Layer: Benioff argued that AI is "probabilistic" and requires a "source of truth," which Salesforce provides through integrated data (evidenced by the $8B-$9B Informatica acquisition).
  • SaaS Survival: Chamath noted that the "low end of the market is finished," but high-end monoliths with deep C-suite relationships remain secure as they become the primary distributors of AI tokens to enterprises.

AI Infrastructure and the OpenAI-Apple Rift

  • Strategic Friction: OpenAI is reportedly considering legal action against Apple due to the failed integration of ChatGPT into Siri.
      • Gripes: OpenAI claims Apple has not promoted the integration and requires users to explicitly name "ChatGPT," limiting subscription conversions.
      • Competition: Tensions are high as OpenAI recruits Apple talent (like Jony Ive) to build competing hardware.
  • Local vs. Cloud AI: Jason Calacanis argued that Apple’s hardware (M5 chips with high RAM) creates a path for local, private models, while Chamath believes "persistence" across devices will keep the advantage with cloud-based models.
  • Multi-Sensory Models: The panel highlighted the transition from text-based LLMs to real-time, multi-sensory models that "watch" desktops and listen to audio every 200 milliseconds, which could increase token consumption by 1,000x.

Global Risks: El Niño and Secondary Markets

  • Climate Crisis (Science Corner): Friedberg warned of a historically strong El Niño in 2026, driven by 11 million terawatt hours of excess energy stored in the oceans.
      • Food Security: High probability of monsoon failure in India and crop failure in Brazil, potentially leading to a global "calorie deficit" and civil unrest in South Asia.
      • Economic Impact: Expect spiking energy prices and grid failures in the U.S. Southwest and Canada.
  • Anthropic’s Secondary Market Crackdown: Anthropic is moving to negate "Dark SPVs" (Special Purpose Vehicles) that charge predatory fees (10% load-in fees) to retail investors.
      • The "Bullet" to SPVs: Chamath praised the move, arguing that multi-layered SPVs are "disgraciad" and that companies should go public sooner to rationalize their equity structures.

Notable Quotes

"I’m not a Democrat or a Republican. I’m an American... I believe business is the greatest platform for change." — Marc Benioff
"The simplest and surest path to a no-conflict détente with China is economic entanglement. It has to be bidirectional." — Chamath Palihapitiya
"We are at the end of the 'hands-on-keyboard' era. Most of our engineers just speak... it's all talk, pedal, and Whisper Flow." — Marc Benioff
"This El Niño event goes beyond an interesting weather anecdote. We are looking at temperatures four degrees above normal—the oceans are the battery of weather, and that energy is about to be released." — David Friedberg

Friedberg, you have been a bit obsessed with this relationship and the bipolar nature of it. What are your thoughts here? And what is the goal for Trump? What is winning for Trump coming out of this? What is winning for Xi? I mean, Xi made comment in his opening remarks that it would be ideal if the United States and China could avoid the Thucydides Trap, which as you'll recall we talked about with Graham Allison and we've talked about several times over the last few years. That as a rising power meets a kind of declining power, there is always some moment where you end up in this kind of state of conflict. And the question is: can the US and China avoid conflict and find a path to cooperation, which has happened a few times in history when we've found ourselves in this moment, but doesn't often happen. And I think the way to kind of think about the opportunity is if you're in a resource-expansive world where you are increasing productivity and increasing production globally at an accelerating pace, perhaps there's less of a reason to have conflict. Perhaps there's a way that both societies, both countries, can increase the quality of life for their people without taking it away from the other side. And in a world where you're more resource-constrained or resource-static, that becomes less possible. You have to fight and grab land and grab territory and grab resources from the other side. And it seems that in this moment when we are seeing these extraordinary technology shifts unlocked by AI and automation and biotech and all of these kind of moments of what could be true abundance ahead of us, it seems like the perfect moment to say hey, maybe the world can be more multipolar. It doesn't need to be unipolar, it doesn't need to be bipolar, but everyone can participate in the expansion of the pie. And I think that that's kind of the idealistic way to look at the opportunity in front of the administration and Chinese leadership right now. That top image is the sea surface temperature anomaly. That means how different the sea surface or the ocean temperatures are. And this is compared to 1877 when we had the biggest El Niño year ever, and I'll explain what that means in a moment, but why this all matters. The reason is the oceans are the battery of weather. What happens at one part of the year is that the oceans absorb heat energy. And absorb, absorb, absorb, get hotter and hotter and hotter and then that heat energy is released into the atmosphere. And then the atmosphere drives the weather events that happen all over the world and it becomes somewhat predictable that you can estimate what the weather over the next year is going to be like based on how the temperatures are sitting in the ocean today. And at this point there is so much excess energy stored up in the oceans. I'll give you guys a statistic, it's about 11 million terawatt hours. The whole planet Earth uses 25,000 terawatt hours in a year. So 11 million extra terawatt hours of energy is currently stored up in this ocean and over the next few months that energy's going to be released into the atmosphere. And that will absolutely—99% confidence—that will make the upcoming year the hottest year on record by far that humans have ever experienced, or at least that we've experienced in recent history where we didn't have data going back a long time. It changes the trade winds, which changes how a lot of the weather evolves from a normal season. It changes the moisture in the atmosphere in certain parts of the world. And I'll just give you some highlights of what this means over the coming year. There will be major atmospheric river events where you just get water dumped on you in the southwest, in California, in the Gulf Coast. You'll have very low snowfall and very high heat waves in the northern part of the US going up to Canada. You guys remember all those fires a couple of years ago in Canada? Trump's China Deals Are Just 'Performance,' Says Ex-US Trade Rep

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·19:40 ·8.1K views · 2026-05-15
Katherine Tai (Former US Trade Representative) Donald Trump (US President) Xi Jinping (President of China)
Former USTR Katherine Tai characterizes recent US-China trade summits as "performance art" that prioritizes corporate symbolism over structural rebalancing.
TL;DR Former USTR Katherine Tai characterizes recent US-China trade summits as "performance art" that prioritizes corporate symbolism over structural rebalancing. She warns that the administration lacks a coherent strategy to address China’s manufacturing overcapacity and its...

TL;DR

Former USTR Katherine Tai characterizes recent US-China trade summits as "performance art" that prioritizes corporate symbolism over structural rebalancing. She warns that the administration lacks a coherent strategy to address China’s manufacturing overcapacity and its weaponization of "choke points" like rare earth minerals.

The "Performance" of Rebalancing

  • Optics vs. Substance: Ambassador Tai argues that the recent summit is a "classical" diplomatic exercise where symbolism is the primary product rather than substantive trade reform.
  • Picking "Winners and Losers": While the administration claims to be "rebalancing" trade, Tai suggests they are simply reshuffling which domestic interests win, often favoring wealthy CEOs and large corporations over the middle class.
  • Underwhelming Purchase Agreements:
      • Boeing Jets: The recent commitment for approximately 150-200 jets is "terrifically modest" compared to the 300 jets purchased in 2017. Many of these orders have been held in abeyance since the 2020 Phase One agreement.
      • Agricultural Volatility: Despite promises to purchase 25 million metric tons of soybeans, American farmers face high anxiety due to unfulfilled Chinese commitments and special trade deals cut with Argentina at the expense of US producers.

Technology Distrust and the AI Divide

  • The Nvidia Standoff: Despite the US "greenlighting" H200 chips for sale to China, Chinese firms are reportedly refusing to buy them. Tai notes this reflects a deep, growing dysfunction and mutual distrust in the relationship.
  • AI Industry Fragmentation:
      • Corporate Absence: Notably, most major AI CEOs (except for Elon Musk) avoided the Beijing trip.
      • Taxpayer Leverage: Tai suggests AI leaders are staying away to maintain a narrative that they require US government subsidies and taxpayer support to outcompete China, which would be undermined by pursuing deals in Beijing.
  • Unclear Principles: Tai expresses concern that the administration has not squared its national security goals with its "deals, deals, deals" rhetoric, leaving observers unclear on the actual principles driving US policy.

China’s Manufacturing Dominance and "Choke Points"

  • The Second "China Shock": China is responding to its internal economic challenges by doubling down on export dominance. This creates a risk of a second wave of industrial displacement in the US, affecting jobs and domestic politics.
  • The Rare Earths Struggle:
      • Historical Context: Tai recalls leading a successful WTO case against China regarding rare earth export restrictions in the early 2010s.
      • Strategic Loss: Despite winning that "battle," Tai admits the US lost the "overall struggle," as China’s dominance in rare earths and EV batteries has only consolidated further.
  • Supply Chain Resilience: Tai argues that 45 years of globalization have allowed China to create "choke points" across multiple strategic sectors. She sees little evidence that the current administration is having the "hard conversations" necessary to build true supply chain resilience.

Tariff Proliferation and the "Busan Truce"

  • Overlapping Trade Actions: The US is currently navigating a "heavy tariff environment" involving Section 301, Section 232, and Section 122 tariffs.
  • New Investigations: USTR recently initiated 76 new Section 301 investigations across 60 trading partners. Two specific investigations target China regarding manufacturing overcapacity and forced labor.
  • Threat to Diplomacy: Tai predicts that if these investigations result in new tariffs, they will inevitably undermine the "Busan truce" and jeopardize future state visits or diplomatic progress.
  • The Tool vs. The Goal: Tai emphasizes that a tariff is merely a tool; she questions whether the administration has a clear objective for what these specific tariffs are meant to accomplish for US competitiveness.
"This is a very obvious performance that is going on on both sides... if you look at the number of jets that haven't been purchased [since 2020], that 150 number looks terrifically modest today." — Katherine Tai
"We clearly won the battle [at the WTO] but are losing the overall struggle because today the United States... [is] actually worse off when it comes to access to Chinese rare earths." — Katherine Tai
"The tariff for me is just a tool to accomplish an objective. And I think that the question for the United States... is: what are all of these tariffs supposed to be accomplishing? And that also is not clear to me." — Katherine Tai
Good to have you with us on the show today. It does seem like this summit has been heavy on optics and symbolism. Has anything really been achieved substantially? I think that, as with many things, but certainly these types of events, the symbolism is probably the product. And what's quite interesting is for all the ways in which the Trump administration and President Trump, especially in this second term, have really broken norms domestically and internationally, this particular trip looks very classical. It looks almost like it could have been any other president's trip to China. Interesting that we heard from Jameson Greer that a lot has been achieved in terms of a rebalancing of trade between the two countries. Is this the new norm? I mean, is it not about expansion of trade? Is it rebalancing, trying to recover what's been lost? I think what is interesting is a lot of people don't fully appreciate when the Trump administration talks about rebalance when it comes to trade. It is another way of saying that they are looking to create new winners and losers—different winners and losers from a long-standing policy here on trade. So, I know they always point to the trade deficit, and that is one indicator and a very easily understandable indicator usually represented by a single number. But when I hear Ambassador Greer talk about the need to rebalance trade between the United States and China, I appreciate that he's talking about more than just the trade volumes. He's talking about the trade volumes, but more than just the trade volumes. And I think that that is actually a really important standard to be applying to this summit and whatever outcomes may be coming from it when it comes to trade. At first glance, it does not look like—from the symbolism around the summit to the personnel that are brought onto the summit, to the private sector delegation that is accompanying the President to this summit—it does not look like a real rebalance is happening. If anything, this looks like more of the same and maybe even a more extreme version of what came before Trump in terms of the US-China trade relationship. Should you be talking about winners and losers when what's been agreed upon back in Busan have not even been fulfilled? If you take a look at the commitment from China to buy 25 million metric tons of soybeans, that has yet to be fulfilled. How do you talk about winners and losers at this stage? I think that's a really good question. And let's talk about who's winning and who's losing. When you look at the soybean trade, I think American soybean farmers are going through an enormous amount of anxiety in this year. Not only is the trade with China uncertain and a point of anxiety, there's been special deals cut with Argentinean farmers, the big Argentina bailout that has come at the expense of American commodity farmers. There is also the overall challenge that we have in terms of rising prices in the United States and then the challenges with access to fertilizer supplies. So, it's one thing to look at a particular metric when it comes to a dollar amount for what China may or may not be committed to purchasing, whether or not they purchase it. It's another thing to put those numbers into the context of what is happening in the US economy and what is happening for the actual producers, consumers, and workers in this economy. You wouldn't know by watching the footage and watching and listening to the talking points coming out of this summit that the US economy for ordinary middle-class people is going through a significant amount of disturbance and turmoil today. Ambassador Tai, you can't help but look at the numbers. I mean, just the deal to do with those Boeing jets, 200, when you know the market was actually looking at 500. So also a far cry from the big purchase that was made back in 2017 when Trump was last in town. That was 300 jets. I mean, how are you reading all these numbers and the kind of commitment perhaps China may or may not be showing in terms of trade with the US? I think that this is a very obvious performance that is going on on both sides, on the US side and the Chinese side, taking place on the stage in Beijing. In terms of those Boeing purchases, China has owed Boeing purchases going all the way back to 2020 and that first phase one agreement that was inked between the Trump administration in its first term and China. Those purchases have been held in abeyance for these past six years. And when you look at that context, then, set aside the expectations around and the comparisons to the number of jets that were purchased before, but if you look at the number of jets that haven't been purchased, that 150 number looks terrifically modest today. It's not just about soybeans and jets. It's also about chips. We know that the addition of NVIDIA CEO Jensen Huang came pretty last minute. We also know that the US has greenlit H200 chips. I mean, how are you reading that, bearing in mind that, you know, the US has talked about national security, and here it is greenlighting the H200? How do you square that? I'm not sure how they square it. And it's the job of this administration and the leaders in terms of economic policy, national security policy, and foreign policy, to square that. It is very unclear to me as an outsider watching the signals what the principles are that this administration is pursuing on behalf of the United States in navigating this enormously consequential but complicated and difficult relationship with the People's Republic of China. There is clearly a need for a reset in terms of the relationship. I think that the overall tone of doing deals, deals, deals and these very, very wealthy CEOs going on this trip to Beijing really undermines any message that this administration has put out there around the hard realities and the hard truths that need to be spoken between Washington and Beijing. And what's also interesting is the fact that despite the US greenlighting the chips, China ain't buying. I mean, it speaks to the deep distrust, doesn't it? It does. It speaks to deep distrust. It speaks to a growing dysfunction in this relationship, which I agree is imbalanced. The People's Republic of China is the world's largest producer and manufacturer and exporter of goods. The United States is the world's largest importer and consumer of goods. When you look at this particular relationship, you see the degree of distortion and mutual reliance that these two economies have on each other. It is driving a very significant economic unhealthiness and political disturbance here domestically in the United States. And I don't see any of those issues on the table being taken up in these conversations with China. Do you see further decoupling between the US and China, especially in technology, and is that decoupling perhaps irreversible now? You know, it's really hard to say whether I see it or not. Again, going back to the lack of clarity around the principles driving this relationship for this administration. I did hear in the clip the piece about the Board of Trade being about US-China conversation around the goods that the United States wants to sell to China. I did not hear Ambassador Greer include chips in his list of products. And yet, and yet there are at least two big semiconductor company CEOs on this particular trip trying to sell their product to the Chinese. And we know that US chips are being used as a leverage for the US. If the Chinese refuses, continue to refuse to buy the H200, do you think the US runs the risk of losing this leverage? I don't know. I mean, I think there's the H200 chips, right? There's the AI chips. Interesting to see that other than Elon Musk, none of the AI CEOs are on this trip. And part of that is because the AI company CEOs are driving a narrative that they need to receive all of the benefits from the US government and the US taxpayer in order to outcompete China. There are a lot of, when you look at the actual signals that are coming out of these few days in Beijing, there's not a clear signal that comes out for discerning people like your Bloomberg viewers. And I don't know what to make of this. And another thing that's not clear is actually rare earths. We've seen China weaponize rare earths, sending the US to its knees when it withheld those supplies. I'm just wondering, do you see China doing that again? That is a tremendous leverage point of holding a choke point in the global economy in a critical area that is affecting so many relevant and upcoming technologies and industries. There's no reason why China would give up that particular leverage from my point of view. And what can the US do? The US has talked about perhaps, you know, producing its own 100,000 batteries annually, which is half of what the world needs in three years. Is that even realistic? I mean, what is the path forward for rare earths for the US? I think it's going to be really hard. We, the United States and the rest of the world, have allowed for China to develop this completely dominant position in the global market on rare earths. And it's not that it's a problem that caught us by surprise. In fact, in the early 2010s, when I was at USTR the first time around as the Chief Counsel for China Trade Enforcement, I led a team that litigated a WTO case, that's at the World Trade Organization, against China on export restrictions the Chinese had placed on rare earths. We won that case, but looking back, we clearly won the battle but are losing the overall struggle. Today the United States and similarly situated countries in the rest of the world are actually worse off when it comes to access to Chinese rare earths or rare earths in general. And I think a really important aspect of this, which speaks to the larger challenge of trade relations with China for the United States and for much of the rest of the world, is that this version of globalization of trade liberalization that we have been pursuing for the last 45 years has resulted in the massive concentration and consolidation, not just in rare earths but across multiple really strategic sectors. China now holds as choke points. And for the rest of us, then, the question is what are the hard truths, the hard conversations we need to have with ourselves and with Beijing in order to evolve our situation so that we can turn down the dial on geopolitical temperatures and pressures so that there can be more resilience and more supply chains that run through more parts of the world. And I think I see evidence of some of that work coming from the Trump administration. It is entirely unclear to me whether any of that is being taken up in these conversations. Do you see a different China? Is this a China that's more confident because it is dominant in the space of rare earths? Again, not just rare earths, right? Solar panels that we've seen over time, EVs and EV batteries. I think this goes to a diagnosis that had come, become very, very clear certainly at the end of the Biden administration, which is around the overbuild up of capacity in China in all kinds of manufacturing sectors. And the linking of the reality today that China is responding to its own economic challenges by doubling down on its dominance as a manufacturer, threatening a second China shock for the United States and the rest of the world with all of the concomitant challenges to jobs, to communities, to industries and to domestic politics. And what I find very interesting is that with all of the rhetorical talk that members of the Trump administration will parrot around advancing economic and trade policies in favor of the working person and working class, none of that is on display. Very little of that conversation around the China shock that is here in its second iteration or the need to respond to this doubling down in terms of China's export dominance—none of that has really been in the mainstream conversation. My fear is that none of that is actually being addressed in Beijing at this opportunity between the two leaders. Ambassador Tai, I also want to touch on the 301 tariffs. What's the status of those tariffs? Are they indeed undermining US competitiveness? Let me answer the first part of your question first, which is there are a lot of 301 tariffs. There are the ones that were applied in 2018. Those were the ones that were China facing. There have been 76 301 investigations that USTR initiated about two months ago in the middle of March, about two weeks after the IPEA tariffs were declared unconstitutional and the Section 122 tariffs were put in their place. My expectation is that these investigations will probably wrap up with remedies announced in time for the Section 122 tariffs to either expire at the end of July or to be declared potentially unconstitutional again by the US court system. When it comes to those new 301 tariffs, again, there are 76 investigations that are looking at 60 different trading partners. China is facing two sets of investigations: one for the overcapacity in manufacturing sectors and the second one for failing to have a forced labor import ban. I think that in your conversation with Ambassador Greer, the question about what should happen if those investigations turn into tariffs and whether or not that will implicate the Busan truce, I think you saw Ambassador Greer really be restrained in his response. But my reading between the lines is that inevitably, should those investigations come out and manifest new tariffs that are directed at China, those absolutely will impact the Busan truce and the dynamics going into any potential fall return state visit. How about in terms of how perhaps the 301 tariffs are making the US less competitive? I mean, it does add to input costs, especially manufacturing and AI. What's your take on that? I don't know how you separate 301 tariffs from the IPEA tariffs from the 232 tariffs. We are living in a very, very heavy tariff environment. And as a former trade representative, as someone who has made my way in the world really focused on trade policy, the tariff for me is just a tool to accomplish an objective. And I think that the question for the United States under this second Trump term today is: what are all of these tariffs supposed to be accomplishing? And that also is not clear to me. Ambassador Tai, just one final question, and in 30 seconds before we wrap it up, I'm just wondering, what's the biggest risk out there in terms of relations between the US and China right now? What keeps you up at night? My concern is that this administration fails to deliver on a coherent policy with clear principles that are keyed to learning lessons from the past. We cannot afford to keep making the same mistakes over and over and over. The mistakes that we've made in the past have led us to today, which is a less peaceful, less and potentially less prosperous world. Ambassador Tai.

Washington Today (5-15-26): Pres. Trump talks about Taiwan, Iran, trade on flight back from China

C-SPAN(1.9M) ·57:55 ·5.0K views · 2026-05-15
Donald Trump (President of the United States) Xi Jinping (President of China) Bill Cassidy (U.S. Senator, Louisiana)
> "Would the U.S.
TL;DR: President Trump returned from a Beijing summit without making explicit commitments regarding Taiwan’s defense or Iran, while the U.S. House passed a $157 billion military spending bill after soundly rejecting a Republican effort to defund NATO infrastructure. Taiwan...

TL;DR: President Trump returned from a Beijing summit without making explicit commitments regarding Taiwan’s defense or Iran, while the U.S. House passed a $157 billion military spending bill after soundly rejecting a Republican effort to defund NATO infrastructure.

Foreign Policy and the China Summit

  • Taiwan Ambiguity: President Trump told reporters aboard Air Force One that he made "no commitment either way" on supporting Taiwan’s independence or defending the island militarily.
      • Arms Sales: Trump is weighing a $14 billion weapons sale to Taiwan, noting he discussed the matter in "great detail" with President Xi Jinping but will make his own decision.
      • Strategic Deterrence: Trump declined to say if the U.S. would intervene in a conflict, stating, "There’s only one person that knows that... Me."
  • Iran and the Strait of Hormuz: The President claimed a U.S. blockade has "essentially wiped out" Iran’s armed forces.
      • China’s Role: Trump stated he did not ask President Xi for "favors" regarding Iran, arguing that since China receives 40% of its oil from the Strait and the U.S. receives none, it is in Xi’s own interest to pressure Iran to keep the waterway open.
  • Economic Agreements: Trump touted "billion-dollar days" in Beijing, including a deal for Boeing to sell China 200 planes (with a promise of up to 750) and hundreds of General Electric engines.
  • Human Rights: Trump reported that President Xi said releasing pro-democracy advocate Jimmy Lai would be "tough," though Xi is "strongly considering" the release of an underground church pastor.

Military Spending and NATO Infrastructure

  • FY2027 Appropriations: The House passed a $157 billion spending bill for military construction and Veterans Affairs in a 400-15 vote, marking the first appropriations bill of the cycle.
  • NATO Funding Dispute: An amendment by Rep. Greg Steube (R-FL) to shift $482 million from NATO infrastructure to domestic U.S. bases failed 80-333.
      • "America First" Argument: Steube argued that allies like France and Spain "subvert" U.S. efforts against Iran by denying airspace and should not receive American taxpayer subsidies.
      • Bipartisan Opposition: Rep. Debbie Wasserman Schultz (D-FL) argued that the cuts would force the U.S. to shoulder the full cost of bases that currently receive 85% of their funding from allies.
  • Army Budget Shortfall: Army Secretary Daniel Driscoll and Acting Chief of Staff Gen. Christopher Leneve denied reports of a $4–$6 billion shortfall requiring training cuts. They attributed budget pressures to the war in Iran and missions at the southern U.S. border but expressed optimism about being "backfilled" by the Department of Homeland Security.

Domestic Politics and Redistricting

  • Louisiana Senate Primary: Incumbent Senator Bill Cassidy (R-LA) faces a high-stakes primary against Trump-endorsed Rep. Julia Letlow and State Treasurer John Fleming.
      • Retribution Tour: Analysts suggest Cassidy is a target for his 2021 vote to convict Trump during the impeachment trial.
      • Logistical Chaos: The primary is being held on a Saturday under a new, "messy" closed system following ongoing legal battles over the state’s redistricting.
  • Tennessee Retirement: Rep. Steve Cohen (D-TN) announced he will not seek re-election after his majority-black district was redrawn by the GOP-controlled legislature. Cohen called the new map a "lion’s mouth," noting it was specifically designed to defeat him.

Public Safety and Health

  • Parental Prosecution in D.C.: U.S. Attorney Janine Pirro announced a new policy to aggressively prosecute parents of minors involved in "teen takeovers" and curfew violations in Washington, D.C.
      • Legal Penalties: Parents could face up to six months in prison for "contributing to the delinquency of a minor" if they fail to supervise their children.
  • Global Virus Outbreaks:
      • Ebola: A new outbreak in the Democratic Republic of Congo has resulted in 246 suspected cases and 65 deaths.
      • Hantavirus: The CDC is monitoring 41 people across 16 U.S. states following an outbreak linked to a South American cruise ship. CDC Director Dr. Jay Bhattacharya emphasized that there are currently no cases within the U.S. and the risk to the public remains "extremely low."

Eighty House Republicans voted on Friday, writes the Washington Examiner, for a measure to cut nearly $482 million from the military budget for the North Atlantic Treaty Organization and put the money into American military bases. The NATO Security Investment Program is a pool of money that all 32 countries in the alliance contribute to, funding digital and infrastructure projects. The U.S. and Germany are the biggest contributors to the program. That was from the Washington Examiner. This amendment was offered today by Congressman Greg Steube, Republican of Florida, to the fiscal year 2027 military construction and Veterans Affairs appropriations bill. It failed 80 to 333. Here's Congressman Steube offering the amendment. "I rise today to offer an amendment that puts the defense of our own troops, bases, and national security above all else. Not to subsidize the defenses of NATO countries that lecture us on peace, hesitate to stand with us, and in some cases actively undermine our efforts to confront the terrorist regime in Iran." "My amendment redirects more than 480 million in funding from the NATO Security Investment Program. Essentially, it moves nearly half a billion dollars used for foreign military base infrastructure and redirects it toward improving the infrastructure needs of our own bases, for our own service members, and for our own national security." "This is the definition of America First. The United States cannot continue carrying the defense burden for allied nations that fail to carry their own weight in the collective defense of each NATO member. But most importantly, we cannot continue to treat the generosity of the American taxpayer as automatic, while our allies treat their obligations as optional." "Operation Epic Fury has exposed why this amendment is necessary. When President Trump moved to confront the Iranian terror regime, some NATO countries chose to tarnish the meaning of alliance and tried to subvert our ability to target Iran." "Namely, it is France and Spain that continue to deny our military from using their airspace and many key defense facilities, forcing American forces to work around the very allied infrastructure that American taxpayers have helped sustain through NATO funding." "Many of these bases exist within the broader NATO architecture supported by programs like the NATO Security Investment Program, which this bill gives nearly half a billion dollars to. Yet when the United States called for support, our so-called allies chose silence over solidarity." "Alliances are not entitlement programs, nor are they blank checks. They are solemn commitments built on mutual defense, sacrifice, and responsibility. So if any foreign nation thinks they are deserving of the United States military and the backing of our taxpayers, best they not demand the comfort of our alliance and reject the courage it requires." Congressman Greg Steube, Republican of Florida, on the House floor. Congresswoman Debbie Wasserman Schultz, Democrat of Florida, spoke against the amendment. "This amendment would completely eliminate funding in the bill for the NATO Security Investment Program. The base bill already underfunds NATO infrastructure by $122 million less compared to the president's budget request." "And even President Trump recognized the value of this program and the return on investment that the NSIP program provides for the American taxpayer. The president's budget requests $604 million for the NATO Security Investment Program, which is the amount required to fully fund America's commitments towards NATO infrastructure investments." "The base bill provides only $482 million for the program, and this amendment would take that number all the way to zero. As the war in Ukraine rages on, this is not the time to abandon deterrence in Europe and undermine our alliances in the region." "Moreover, these investments are not only needed to meet NATO's requirements to deter aggression from our adversaries, but also to directly support U.S. troops stationed in Europe." "Around one-third of active NATO Security Investment Program projects are at U.S. theater posture locations, and an even larger number indirectly support U.S. troops stationed in or moving throughout the region. Yet the United States' share of funding for these projects is only 15 percent." "If the United States were to pay a share proportional to our gross national income, at the same rate as our allies, we would pay for around 47 percent of these projects. To cut funding for this program is not only not fiscally sound policy." "Without the NATO Security Investment Program, the operational requirements for these projects would not go away, and many of these projects would become a U.S. military construction burden." Congresswoman Debbie Wasserman Schultz, Democrat of Florida, on the House floor. This amendment failed by a vote of 80 "yes" and 333 "no." All 80 "yeses" were from Republicans, and on the "no" side, 127 Republicans, 205 Democrats, and the one independent, Kevin Kiley of California. The underlying bill, the fiscal year 2027 appropriations bill for military construction and Veterans Affairs Department medical care and related agencies, is $157 billion, and that did pass today, 400 to 15. That vote—all 15 "no" votes came from Democrats. House Speaker Mike Johnson, Republican of Louisiana, spoke about the bill's passage at a news conference. "Today we just passed our first regular order appropriations bill for the FY fiscal year '27 cycle. This is again fulfilling our commitment to get back to regular order in the appropriations process." "That's the only way for Congress to be a good steward of taxpayer funds. We have broken the Christmas omnibus spending bill madness that has beset Congress for many, many years, and we're getting back, rebuilding the muscle memory for Congress to do its job. We’re really proud of that." "Now we have the remaining appropriations bills that will be coming through in short order, trying to keep on the clock and make sure this happens well before the end of the fiscal year. So stay tuned for more of that action." House Speaker Mike Johnson, Republican of Louisiana, at a news conference. The new fiscal year begins October 1st. CBS News writing the top federal prosecutor in the nation's capital announced on Friday her office is planning to target parents if their teenagers violate the local curfew in a move aimed at curbing the growing problem of teen takeovers. Teen takeovers have become a frequent nuisance in Washington, D.C., particularly in popular parts of the city like the Navy Yard Waterfront. At times, these flash mob-style gatherings involving large groups of teens have escalated, prompting D.C. Mayor Muriel Bowser to impose a series of juvenile curfews. In the Navy Yard area, it is 11 p.m. for those under the age of 18. That was from CBS News. Janine Pirro is the U.S. attorney for the District of Columbia. She held a news conference today. "There is one area in Washington, D.C., that has seen more than its share of one problem: teen takeovers. These takeovers have terrorized our neighborhoods, they have shut down businesses, and they have wasted hard-earned tax dollars of law-abiding residents who just want to live and work in peace." "Those takeovers in places like the Navy Yard and NoMa often lead to disorderly conduct, assaults, robberies, and fights. Mayor Muriel Bowser has repeatedly requested curfew zones and emergency measures while the D.C. Council refuses to deal with the problem." "And I'm going to say that again. Where the D.C. Council that passes the laws refuses to deal with the problem. And their lack of action creates an extremely dangerous situation for the people of the District and even the teens themselves." "And as we grapple with this problem, there is one area that hasn't been discussed: parental involvement has been a noted gap in any discussion. And I am here to say, as the United States attorney in the District of Columbia, that ends today." "Starting today, my office will aggressively prosecute parents under D.C.'s curfew law. And the specific statute that we will use is a violation of D.C. Code 22-811, and it involves contributing to the delinquency of a minor." "This statute makes it unlawful for an adult to enable, facilitate, or permit a minor to engage in delinquent acts. The penalty is up to six months' imprisonment. So if the evidence shows that parents knew or should have known or permitted or failed to prevent participation, we're going to charge them." "And if you drop your kid off and you fail to supervise them, or you let them skip school to join the chaos, you are going to face fines, court-ordered classes, and possible jail time. Penalties can be imposed even if the juvenile minor is not prosecuted." Janine Pirro, U.S. attorney for the District of Columbia, at a news conference today. Here is Governor Spanberger with that swamp story. "Which reminds me of the time that I ended up in a swamp. Stick with me on this one. That wasn't a political reference, I mean a literal swamp. Early in my career at CIA, I went on one of my many training exercises, and the premise was very simple." "My colleagues and I needed to get from Point A to Point B. Our training facilitators gave us a compass, lined us up along the woods, and said, 'Now you wait.' We didn't know what we were waiting for, we didn't know where we were going, we only knew the azimuth on that old-school compass we needed to set." "It got dark, night fell. All of a sudden we’re standing on the edge of the woodline and they said, 'Go.' And we said, 'Where?' And they said, 'Follow your compass.' And that’s it. Figure it out." "So we set off individually, all knowing that our other colleagues were somewhere in the woods as well, but it was dark and it was summer, so it was hot and the trees were dense. And we set off, and at one point I noticed the ground below me kind of getting softer, noticed I was on a decline, and then I realized I was walking into a swamp." "And I kept sort of walking through. At what point in time is this not a good idea? In the dark, in a swamp, and there’s noises and crinkling, and I’m just staring at these little glow-in-the-dark lines on my compass." And I kept thinking maybe I should turn back." "But I kept going, we kept going, and that water only came up mid-shin and so I kept going. And I’ll be honest, trudging through a swamp in the dark with just a little glow-in-the-dark line on a compass, that was never a specific skill that I was going to need in real life. But the whole point of the training wasn't that destination." It was proving to myself, my colleagues, proving to themselves that—that you can do hard things and that quite literally you follow the compass, whether it’s the one in your hand or the one in your heart or the one in your mind, the compass leading you towards what it is that you wish to be doing. And so on your hardest days, wherever you are, whatever is challenging you, you will be able to draw on memories of things you have overcome, challenges you have faced. In my case, it’s the swamp. But yours might be a thesis you almost abandoned, a course that was particularly challenging, a semester that tested your limits, or an over-commitment to far too many activities where you wondered how you could handle it all. But Virginia Tech has been building these memories for you for four years. And that is part of what your diploma will represent: a record of the hard things that you have done, that you have gotten through, and places where you have stayed, following that inner compass because you are sitting here today. So take those memories with you. They are more portable than you think. Why This Market “Melt-Up” is Making Me Nervous | The Weekly Wrap

Steve Eisman(174.0K) ·21:32 ·58.1K views · 2026-05-15
Steve Eisman (Portfolio Manager & Host)
Steve Eisman warns that the current market "melt-up" has become dangerously frothy, characterized by extreme concentration in AI-related semiconductors, the disappearance of defensive sectors, and rising inflation data that may threaten the sustainability of recent gains.
TL;DR Steve Eisman warns that the current market "melt-up" has become dangerously frothy, characterized by extreme concentration in AI-related semiconductors, the disappearance of defensive sectors, and rising inflation data that may threaten the sustainability of recent gains. ...

TL;DR

Steve Eisman warns that the current market "melt-up" has become dangerously frothy, characterized by extreme concentration in AI-related semiconductors, the disappearance of defensive sectors, and rising inflation data that may threaten the sustainability of recent gains.

Market Overheating and Macro Risks

  • Signs of "Froth": Eisman expresses nervousness regarding the current rally, noting that the S&P 500 is up 9% and the Nasdaq is up 15% in a little over a month.
      • Sector Concentration: Semiconductors now represent over 18% of the S&P 500. Notably, NVIDIA's market cap now exceeds the entire healthcare sector.
      • Vanishing Defensives: The combined weight of the four defensive sectors (Healthcare, Consumer Staples, Energy, and Utilities) has plummeted from 31% at the end of 2022 to just 19% today.
  • Retail Sentiment: Retail traders are at their most bullish since 2021, aggressively buying calls on the "Mag 10" stocks (the Magnificent Seven plus AMD, Palantir, and Broadcom).
  • Inflationary Pressures: The Producer Price Index (PPI) rose 1.4% in April, far exceeding the 0.5% forecast. On an annual basis, PPI is up 6%, the highest since late 2022.
      • Interest Rates: The 10-year yield is flirting with 4.5%, a level Eisman describes as a "Rubicon" for the markets.

Private Credit and "A Conspiracy of Credit"

  • Systemic Risks: Eisman is launching a masterclass focusing on the "veil of secrecy" in private credit. He draws parallels between current private credit growth and the toxic mortgage bonds of the Great Financial Crisis (GFC).
  • FS KKR Capital (FSK) Warning: This public BDC is a "canary in the coal mine."
      • Credit Cuts: JPMorgan and a group of banks slashed FSK’s credit line by $650 million (14%) and raised interest rates on the remainder. Eisman notes that credit line cuts for financial services firms are a major red flag.
      • Loan Markdowns: According to MSCI, private credit funds have already marked down more than 10% of their loans by at least 50%.
  • Outlook: While Eisman expects significant losses and a potential recession in this space, he believes a total disaster is unlikely because banks remain well-capitalized.

Mixed Earnings and Sector Volatility

  • The Payment Space: Eisman describes investing here as "rolling dice" due to hyper-competition.
      • Toast: Dropped 17% following weak EBITDA guidance despite an earnings beat.
      • Bill & Block: Both saw gains after significant workforce reductions (30-40%) to pivot toward AI and profitability, though both remain volatile.
  • AI and Infrastructure:
      • Cisco: A major winner, up double digits after reporting 12% revenue growth and strong AI-related orders.
      • CoreWeave: Despite revenue more than doubling, the stock dropped 8% due to a miss on forward revenue guidance. Eisman notes that "money-losing" companies cannot afford to miss on any metric.
      • Constellation Energy (CEG): Reported 28% growth due to the AI data center boom but saw a negative stock reaction as management left full-year guidance unchanged.
  • Travel and Tech Softness:
      • Expedia: Down 9% due to weak booking forecasts and "geopolitical uncertainty."
      • The Trade Desk: Once a high-flyer, the stock is down 46% this year as revenue growth slowed from 20% to 8%, driven by competition from Amazon.

Individual Positions and Special Situations

  • FICO Short: Eisman remains short on FICO, citing its 1,500% price hikes over five years and its eroding monopoly status as regulators look for cheaper scoring alternatives.
  • Homebuilders: Despite being bearish on FICO, Eisman remains long on Meritage Homes, arguing that buying a homebuilder below tangible book value is a sound long-term strategy, even if rising rates delay the payoff.
  • eBay/GameStop: eBay rejected GameStop’s takeover offer, questioning the financing. Eisman characterized Ryan Cohen’s recent public behavior regarding the offer as "bizarre."

Notable Quotes

"The sum of the four defensive sectors—healthcare, consumer staples, energy, and utilities—represents only 19% of the S&P 500. That number was 31% at the end of 2022. Defensive sectors seem to be disappearing before our eyes." — Steve Eisman
"Almost all the growth in lending since 2008 has been in private credit behind a veil of privacy, which means secrecy... History seems to be repeating itself." — Steve Eisman
"When a financial services company has its credit lines cut, that is always really, really bad." — Steve Eisman
"I have to admit, I am nervous about the sustainability of this melt-up... This week, I lightened up, especially on my high flyers, because this market is feeling bubbly." — Steve Eisman

The market is now up 9%, and Nasdaq is up 15%. It's feeling frothy. Part of the rally is an assumption by the market that the war is over. And I'm just not sure that is right. I have to admit, I am nervous about the sustainability of this melt-up because this market is feeling bubbly. And that makes me nervous.
